<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="High-quality genome sequence of white lupin provides insight into soil exploration and seed quality"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-01-24"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="White lupin (Lupinus albus L.) is an annual crop cultivated for its protein-rich seeds. It is adapted to poor soils due to the production of cluster roots, which are made of dozens of determinate lateral roots that drastically improve soil exploration and nutrient acquisition (mostly phosphate). Using long-read sequencing technologies, we provide a high-quality genome sequence of a cultivated accession of white lupin (2n&#8201;=&#8201;50, 451&#8201;Mb), as well as de novo assemblies of a landrace and a wild relative. We describe a modern accession displaying increased soil exploration capacity through early establishment of lateral and cluster roots. We also show how seed quality may have been impacted by domestication in term of protein profiles and alkaloid content. The availability of a high-quality genome assembly together with companion genomic and transcriptomic resources will enable the development of modern breeding strategies to increase and stabilize white lupin yield. White lupin is an annual crop cultivated for protein rich seeds and can produce cluster roots for efficient phosphate acquisition. Here, the authors generate high quality genome assemblies of a cultivated accession, a landrace, and a wild relative and provides insight into soil exploration and seed quality."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>White lupin (<i>Lupinus albus L</i>.) is an annual crop cultivated for its protein-rich seeds. It is adapted to poor soils due to the production of cluster roots, which are made of dozens of determinate lateral roots that drastically improve soil exploration and nutrient acquisition (mostly phosphate). Using long-read sequencing technologies, we provide a high-quality genome sequence of a cultivated accession of white lupin (2n = 50, 451 Mb), as well as de novo assemblies of a landrace and a wild relative. We describe a modern accession displaying increased soil exploration capacity through early establishment of lateral and cluster roots. We also show how seed quality may have been impacted by domestication in term of protein profiles and alkaloid content. The availability of a high-quality genome assembly together with companion genomic and transcriptomic resources will enable the development of modern breeding strategies to increase and stabilize white lupin yield.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Lupins are commonly known as beautiful ornamental plants, bearing numerous colorful flowers. These plants belong to the <i>Lupinus</i> genus that is richly diverse with more than 300 species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Drummond, C. S., Eastwood, R. J., Miotto, S. T. S. &amp; Hughes, C. E. Multiple continental radiations and correlates of diversification in Lupinus (Leguminosae): testing for key innovation with incomplete taxon sampling. Syst. Biol. 61, 443–460 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15561e854">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hughes, C. &amp; Eastwood, R. Island radiation on a continental scale: exceptional rates of plant diversification after uplift of the Andes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 10334–10339 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d15561e857">2</a></sup>. They are grouped into Old World lupins (Mediterranean) and New World lupins (American) and display a remarkable array of ecological habitats, justifying their interest as a case study for genome evolution, adaptation and speciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hughes, C. &amp; Eastwood, R. Island radiation on a continental scale: exceptional rates of plant diversification after uplift of the Andes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 103, 10334–10339 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d15561e861">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ainouche, A.-K. &amp; Bayer, R. J. Phylogenetic relationships in Lupinus (Fabaceae: Papilionoideae) based on internal transcribed spacer sequences (ITS) of nuclear ribosomal DNA. Am. J. Bot. 86, 590–607 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d15561e864">3</a></sup>. Among them, white lupin (<i>Lupinus albus L</i>.) is a pulse that originates from the Mediterranean region, its center of origin is believed to be Greece, Western Turkey and southern Balkans where wild ‘<i>graecus</i>’ types still persist<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gladstones, J. S. Lupins as Crop Plants: Biology, Production and Utilization (eds Gladstones J. S., Atkins C, Hamblin J.) 1–39 (CAB International, Oxon, New York, 1998)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d15561e875">4</a></sup>. This crop is recognized as a traditional food due to its very high protein content (between 30 and 40% of the whole seed)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Bähr, M., Fechner, A., Hasenkopf, K., Mittermaier, S. &amp; Jahreis, G. Chemical composition of dehulled seeds of selected lupin cultivars in comparison to pea and soya bean. LWT Food Sci. Technol. 59, 587–590 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d15561e879">5</a></sup>.</p><p>Cultivation of white lupin (WL) started around 4000 years ago but modern breeding efforts have been very limited and focused on a few major traits such as permeable seeds, early flowering, non-shattering pods, and low alkaloid seed content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wolko, B., Clements, J. C., Naganowska, B., Nelson, M. N. &amp; Yang, H. Lupinus. Wild Crop Relatives: Genomic and Breeding Resources. 153–206 (Springer Berlin Heidelberg, 2011). ." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d15561e886">6</a></sup>. WL cultivation has the potential to solve several issues related to the future of European protein supply due to its high quality seeds (very high levels of proteins, high levels of tocopherols, lowest glycemic index of consumed grains, high dietary fiber content, gluten-free, low oil, and minimal starch)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Bähr, M., Fechner, A., Hasenkopf, K., Mittermaier, S. &amp; Jahreis, G. Chemical composition of dehulled seeds of selected lupin cultivars in comparison to pea and soya bean. LWT Food Sci. Technol. 59, 587–590 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d15561e890">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Boschin, G., D’Agostina, A., Annicchiarico, P. &amp; Arnoldi, A. Effect of genotype and environment on fatty acid composition of Lupinus albus L. seed. Food Chem. 108, 600–606 (2008)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d15561e893">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fontanari, G. G. et al. Cholesterol-lowering effect of whole lupin (Lupinus albus) seed and its protein isolate. Food Chem. 132, 1521–1526 (2012)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d15561e893_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Boschin, G. &amp; Arnoldi, A. Legumes are valuable sources of tocopherols. Food Chem. 127, 1199–1203 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d15561e896">9</a></sup>. It is also a crop with low need for phosphate fertilizers due to its highly adapted root system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Lucas, M. M. et al. The future of lupin as a protein crop in Europe. Front. Plant Sci. 6, 705 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d15561e900">10</a></sup> and no need of nitrogen input as a legume.</p><p>White Lupin is one of the few crops that can produce spectacular structures called cluster roots, harboring a specific physiology dedicated towards efficient Pi acquisition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lambers, H., Clements, J. C. &amp; Nelson, M. N. How a phosphorus-acquisition strategy based on carboxylate exudation powers the success and agronomic potential of lupines (Lupinus, Fabaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 263–288 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d15561e907">11</a></sup>. Despite being an essential micronutrient, inorganic phosphate is poorly available in the soil and plants have developed various strategies to improve Pi remobilization and acquisition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Giehl, R. F. H., Gruber, B. D. &amp; von Wirén, N. It’s time to make changes: modulation of root system architecture by nutrient signals. J. Exp. Bot. 65, 769–778 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d15561e911">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lynch, J. P. Root phenes for enhanced soil exploration and phosphorus acquisition: tools for future crops. Plant Physiol. 156, 1041–1049 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d15561e914">13</a></sup>. Most terrestrial plants form mycorrhizal symbiosis to improve soil exploration but WL has lost the ability to form such associations. Instead, by producing cluster roots, WL can take up almost 5 times more Pi per root length unit than soybean, a mycorrhizal legume that does not form cluster roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Watt, M. &amp; Evans, J. R. Phosphorus acquisition from soil by white lupin (Lupinus albus L.) and soybean (Glycine max L.), species with contrasting root development. Plant Soil 248, 271–283 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d15561e918">14</a></sup>, suggesting a strong potential for crop improvement towards better nutrient acquisition efficiency<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lambers, H., Clements, J. C. &amp; Nelson, M. N. How a phosphorus-acquisition strategy based on carboxylate exudation powers the success and agronomic potential of lupines (Lupinus, Fabaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 263–288 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d15561e922">11</a></sup>.</p><p>In this study, we present a high-quality genome sequence of a modern accession of white lupin (2n = 50, 451 Mb), as well as de novo assemblies of a landrace and a wild accession. This quality reference sequence allows us to perform in-depth analysis of repetitive elements, to analyze genomic variations across 15 accessions, and to retrace the paleohistory of legumes. We then provide a comparison of soil exploration capacity between a cultivated and a wild accession, highlighting the early establishment of lateral and cluster roots in the modern cultivar. We also provide information regarding seed quality, demonstrating that modern accessions accumulate specific types of conglutins. Finally, we provide a list of candidate genes present in the <i>pauper</i> locus, which is a common QTL controlling the accumulation of toxic alkaloids in WL seeds.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>We generated 164x sequencing coverage of the genome of <i>Lupinus albus</i> cv. AMIGA using 30 single-molecule real-time (SMRT) cells on PacBio Sequel platform. The production of 94 Gb of very long reads along with a depth of 208× (119 Gb) of Illumina 150 bp paired-end sequences for the assembly polishing and with the addition of Bionano optical map technology allowed a genome assembly of 451 Mb. The contig sequences obtained by a meta assembly strategy based on CANU<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k -mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d15561e947">15</a></sup> and FALCON<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d15561e951">16</a></sup> were scaffolded in a first step using a Bionano optical map and in a second step using a high density genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Książkiewicz, M. et al. A high-density consensus linkage map of white lupin highlights synteny with narrow-leafed lupin and provides markers tagging key agronomic traits. Sci. Rep. 7, 15335 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d15561e955">17</a></sup>. The chromosome-level assembly (termed Lalb, Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">1</a>) covers the 25 nuclear chromosomes along with mitochondrial and chloroplastic genomes, leaving only 64 unanchored contigs (8.8 Mb - 2% of the assembly). The maximum number of sequence gaps is four (on chromosomes 10 and 11) and ten chromosomes contain only a single sequence gap, illustrating the high and homogenous contiguity across chromosomes (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the white lupin genome and gene models prediction.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We generated RNA-seq data from ten different organs, widely covering gene expression in WL (entire root system in +Pi and –Pi conditions, lateral roots, primary roots, cluster roots, nodulated root system, leaves, flowers, pods, and seeds). The assembled reads were mapped using EuGene-EP pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Sallet, E., Gouzy, J. &amp; Schiex, T. Gene Prediction (ed Kollmar, M.) 97–120 (Springer New York, 2019). &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/978-1-4939-9173-0_6&#xA;&#xA;." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1072">18</a></sup> and protein and non-protein coding gene models were predicted. Three protein databases (Swiss-Prot, a plant subset of Uniprot proteins and the proteome of <i>Medicago truncatula</i>) were aligned to contribute to translated regions detection. Genome annotation identified 38,258 protein-coding genes and 3129 non-protein-coding genes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">2</a>). Evidence of transcription was found for 92% of the annotated genes. Quality of the annotation was evaluated with a Benchmarking of Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 543–548 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1085">19</a></sup>) analysis, yielding a completeness score of 97.7%. The WL Genome portal (<a href="http://www.whitelupin.fr">www.whitelupin.fr</a>) provides a genome browser and several other user-friendly tools for molecular analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repetitive elements and structure of centromeric regions</h3><p>De novo identification of repeated elements revealed a highly repetitive genome (60%), with over 75% repeats matching known transposable elements (TEs, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1a</a>, Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">4</a>). Chromosomal scale genome-wide annotation of repetitive sequences revealed the in silico annotation of the main classes of repeats (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1b</a>). TEs were most commonly long terminal repeats (LTRs) retrotransposons (34%), with remarkable accumulation of Ty3/gypsy Tekay, CRM chromoviruses and Ty1/copia SIRE towards the central regions of chromosome assemblies along presumed (peri)centromeric regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1b</a>). Class II TEs accounted for ca. 0.8% of the genome (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>) and is in accordance to the lower abundance of this class of repeats in other legume species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Macas, J. et al. In depth characterization of repetitive DNA in 23 plant genomes reveals sources of genome size variation in the legume tribe fabeae. PLoS ONE 10, 1–23 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1121">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hane, J. K. et al. A comprehensive draft genome sequence for lupin (Lupinus angustifolius), an emerging health food: insights into plant-microbe interactions and legume evolution. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 318–330 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1124">21</a></sup>. A high amount of satellite DNA (satDNA) sequences was found, comprising ~15% of the genome (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">5</a>). A narrow peak for the distribution of CRM (Centromeric Retrotransposon of Maize) clade retroelements was observed in all assembled chromosomes, we therefore presumed that the observed peak defines the centromeric regions of WL chromosomes. Thus, we refer to this element as CRWL for Centromeric Retrotransposon of While Lupin. Remarkably, a high association of CRWL and satellite DNA peaks was observed, suggesting a more specific distribution of these repeats compared to other TEs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1b, c</a>) whereas a more diverse distribution of repetitive elements towards the peri-centromeric regions of WL chromosomes was observed (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">2</a>). To further characterize the repeat portion of WL genome we performed in situ hybridization of the most abundant repeat clusters identified by the RepeatExplorer analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1c</a>). As expected, CRWL FISH signals were observed as narrow distributed signals at the centromeric regions of most WL chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1c</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">2</a>), giving an indication of functional centromeres positioning.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Repeated elements abundance in white lupin genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Repeated elements abundance in white lupin genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="541" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Proportion of single copy and repeated sequences for the different classes of repeats. <b>b</b> Density distribution along the chromosomes of the repetitive classes. A. genes, B. CRM, C. satDNA, D. Tekay, and E. SIRE. Density is represented in 0.5 Mb bins. <b>c</b> FISH mapping of the main repeats by super-resolution microscopy (3D-SIM). (1) Co-localization of CRWL and CL2 satDNA on centromeric regions of meiotic (pachytene) chromosomes. (2–3) Distribution of the most abundant satellite DNAs CL1, CL2, and CL10 in somatic metaphase chromosomes. Bars are 10 µm (1) and 2 µm (2 and 3). <b>d</b> LalbCENH3-ChIPseq reads mapped against the first 100 RepeatExplorer clusters of the WL genome. The main centromeric sequences found in LalbCENH3-ChIPseq are highlighted. <b>e</b> Typical centromere composition of a WL chromosome, chromosome 14. Source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1d</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Raising an anti-LalbCENH3 specific antibody, we mapped functional centromeres using immunostaining (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>) and performed LalbCENH3-ChIPseq confirming the association of CRWL main clusters (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1d</a>) with functional centromeres. Analysis of ChIPseq reads demonstrated that CRWL elements (CL13, CL20, CL34, CL48, and CL49) are among the clusters that showed the highest levels of association in the immunoprecipitated fraction (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1d</a>). Although CRWL is highly abundant on centromeric regions of WL chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1b, c</a>), detailed analysis of ChIPseq data revealed that CENH3-containing chromatin is also associated with at least four families of centromeric tandem repeats: CL2-5bp, CL10-78bp, CL21-38bp and CL55-8bp (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1c, d</a>). Super-resolution microscopy of pachytene and somatic chromosomes confirmed a centromere-specific localization for CRWL, CL2, CL10, CL21, and CL55 repeats, while CL1 repeat localizes aside core centromeres (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1c</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">2</a> arrowheads). The total amount of cenDNA represents about 11% (49.55 Mb) of the genome. In contrast, the most abundant satDNA CL1-170bp did not show significant enrichment with the immunoprecipitated DNA, suggesting that this element is excluded from functional centromeres. A typical (peri)centromeric region of a WL chromosome contains the most abundant CL1-170bp repeats representing 18% of the region. These sequences are organized in blocks separated by SIRE retrotransposons. Centromere-associated satellite repeats are present in shorter arrays such as CL2-5bp and CL10-78bp intermingled with CRWL elements (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig1">1e</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">6</a>). Thus, the functional centromeres of WL are preferentially associated with CRWL and with different families of tandem repeats in a chromosome-specific pattern. These results identify a specific centromeric sequence pattern with a highly diverse structure in WL that strongly differs from known centromeric sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">White lupin diversity and genomic structural variations</h3><p>To provide a first overview of WL diversity and possible domestication patterns we re-sequenced 14 WL accessions, including 11 modern accessions, 1 landrace and 2 wild relatives (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">8</a>). The accessions presented a total of 2,659,837 SNPs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig2">2a</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">9</a>) when compared to the reference genome. Pairwise dissimilarities analyses allowed the identification of three clusters reflecting white lupin recent breeding history: winter accessions (vernalization responsive, slow growth, cold adapted), spring accessions (vernalization unresponsive, fast growth, strong vigor, and reduced life-cycle) and landraces/wild types (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig2">2b</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="White Lupin diversity and evolution history."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: White Lupin diversity and evolution history.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="632" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> SNP density identified by resequencing of 15 accessions of white lupin. In the outer track (A in gray) gene density is shown. Spring accessions are represented in green (B–H), winter accessions are represented in blue (I–M) and wild/landrace accessions are represented in red (N-P). From the outer to inner track: (B) AMIGA, (C) FEODORA, (D) KIEV, (E) DIETA, (F) FIGARO, (G) ENERGY, (H) LUCKY, (I) ORUS, (J) CLOVIS, (K) MAGNUS, (L) ULYSSE, (M) LUXE, (N) P27174, (O) GR38, AND (P) GRAECUS. The SNP density is represented in 1 Mb bins. <b>b</b> Neighbor-joining phylogenetic tree of white lupin accessions based on SNPs. The 15 accessions are divided in three clades: winter, spring and wild/landrace. <b>c</b> Admixture representation of the 15 accessions with population clustering for <i>K</i> = 2–5. Each individual is represented by a horizontal bar and each color represents a subpopulation. The color of each individual accession represents their proportional membership in the different populations. <b>d</b> Structural variants (SVs) between <i>L. albus</i> cv. AMIGA and the de novo assembly of GRAECUS (left) and P27174 (right). The biggest proportion of variants are the repeated elements. SVs represent 18.08 Mb of the GRAECUS genome and 18.67 Mb of P27174 genome. Source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig2">2b</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further verify the clustering observed in the phylogenetic tree, a principal component analysis (PCA) was conducted using the same samples and SNP set. More than half of total genetic variance (58.2%) could be explained by the two first components, which replicates the phylogenetic tree results (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>The population structure was explored with the same set of SNPs using STRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Pritchard, J. K., Stephens, M. &amp; Donnelly, P. Inference of population structure using multilocus genotype data. Genetics 155, 945–959 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1279">22</a></sup>. We tested for a population structure ranging from 2 subpopulations (<i>K</i> = 2) up to 5 subpopulations (<i>K</i> = 5, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig2">2c</a>). Additionally, a Evanno’s test<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Evanno, G., Regnaut, S. &amp; Goudet, J. Detecting the number of clusters of individuals using the software STRUCTURE: a simulation study. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2611–2620 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1292">23</a></sup> indicated that these 15 WL accessions might be divided into two subpopulations, one formed by the spring accessions and the other with the winter and non-domesticated accessions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><p>We estimated the level of linkage disequilibrium (LD) using the <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> parameter between all pairwise SNP comparisons by using these 15 accessions in the 25 chromosomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">6</a>). We used a subset of 46,783 high-quality genomic-random distributed SNPs. LD decay distance showed apparent variation, indicating that selection pressure in different chromosomal regions varied, probably due to different selection goals in breeding.</p><p>We selected the wild accession GRAECUS and an Ethiopian landrace (P27174) to further investigate the possible impact of domestication on WL genome. We sequenced these two genotypes using Nanopore long-read technology, at a depth of 27.6x and 32.4x for GRAECUS and P27174, respectively, and generated de novo assemblies (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">10</a>). Using Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021–3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1317">24</a></sup> (based on whole genomes alignments generated with MUMmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1321">25</a></sup>), we identified a high level of structural variations (SVs, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig2">2d</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>). This analysis reveals genomic regions that are strongly altered between the modern accession AMIGA and the two accessions that have not undergone a breeding program. P27174 assembly has a total length of 18.67 Mb of structural variations (SVs) affected and the GRAECUS accession was similarly affected by SVs (18.08 MB – Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig2">2d</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>The majority of the SVs in both GRAECUS and P27174 are located in intergenic regions (62 and 53%, respectively). Considering a promoter region of 2 Kb upstream of 5′-UTR, a total of 8166 genes are impacted by SVs in the GRAECUS genome, whereas only 6524 genes are impacted in P27174. A total of 3463 common genes are altered in both accessions and 671 of these genes have common exons impacted (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">7</a>). These SVs highlight genomic regions that may help understand major events associated with WL domestication.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">White lupin genome evolution</h3><p>We retraced the paleohistory of 12 legume genomes including WL and covering the Genistoid, Dalbergioid, Galegoid, and Millettoid clades. Independent blocks of synteny (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">5</a>) allowed the identification of an ancestral legume karyotype (ALK) made of 16 conserved ancestral regions (CARs), Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">4</a>. The ancestral genome consists of a minimal shared ancestral genome, which lacks components of the ‘real’ (unknown) ancestral genome that were either lost from all of the investigated descendants and/or retained by only one modern species (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">4</a>). This reveals specific rearrangements (chromosome fusions and fissions) and polyploidization events in the case of soybean and lupins (WL and narrow-leafed lupin, NLL), so that modern legume genomes are composed of a mosaic of 16 shuffled CARs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">8</a>). ALK experienced 15 chromosomal fissions and 21 fusions to reach a lupin ancestor of 9 chromosomes that experienced a whole genome triplication to reach a <i>n</i> = 27 ancestor intermediate. The modern karyotypes of WL and NLL evolved from the lupin ancestors through 17 major chromosomal shuffling events followed by numerous small-scale rearrangements such as inversions and translocations (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig3">3b</a>). This comparative genomics-based evolutionary scenario unravels the complex legume paleohistory from the reconstructed ALK, revising previous inferences of legume genomes synteny in delivering the complete catalog of paralogous and orthologous gene relationships between 12 modern legume genomes as well as the ancestral genomes of this major botanical family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Hane, J. K. et al. A comprehensive draft genome sequence for lupin (Lupinus angustifolius), an emerging health food: insights into plant-microbe interactions and legume evolution. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 318–330 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1374">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lee, C., Yu, D., Choi, H.-K. &amp; Kim, R. W. Reconstruction of a composite comparative map composed of ten legume genomes. Genes Genomics 39, 111–119 (2017)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1377">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, J. et al. Hierarchically aligning 10 legume genomes establishes a family-level genomics platform. Plant Physiol. 174, 284 LP–284300 (2017)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1377_1">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhuang, W. et al. The genome of cultivated peanut provides insight into legume karyotypes, polyploid evolution and crop domestication. Nat. Genet. 51, 865–876 (2019)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1377_2">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kreplak, J. et al. A reference genome for pea provides insight into legume genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 51, 1411–1422 (2019)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1377_3">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea. Nat. Genet. 51, 877–884 (2019)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1377_4">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ren, L., Huang, W. &amp; Cannon, S. B. Reconstruction of ancestral genome reveals chromosome evolution history for selected legume species. N. Phytol. 223, 2090–2103 (2019)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1377_5">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kroc, M., Koczyk, G., Święcicki, W., Kilian, A. &amp; Nelson, M. N. New evidence of ancestral polyploidy in the Genistoid legume Lupinus angustifolius L. (narrow-leafed lupin). Theor. Appl. Genet. 127, 1237–1249 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1380">32</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="White Lupin genome evolution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: White Lupin genome evolution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="322" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Legumes evolutionary history. Evolutionary scenario of the modern legumes (white and narrow-leafed lupin, garden pea, peanut, Lotus, barrel medic, chickpea, pigeonpea, soybean, common bean, mungbean, and adzuki bean) from the reconstructed ancestral legume karyotype (ALK, center). The modern genomes are illustrated with different colors reflecting the origin from the ancestral chromosomes. Polyploidization events are shown with red (duplication) and blue (triplication) dots on the tree branches. <b>b</b> TOP- Evolution of white and narrow-leafed lupin genomes from ALK (with 16 CARs) and the lupin ancestral genomes (with 9 and 27 CARS excluding the small CAR #7 in ALK). BOTTOM- Synteny relationships between white (horizontal) and narrow-leafed (vertical) lupin illuminating 17 major chromosomal shuffling events (red and blue arrows for respectively narrow-leafed lupin compared to white lupin and white lupin compared to narrow-leafed lupin).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>An intragenomic analysis for segmental duplications (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig4">4a</a>, Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">5</a>) identified 928 blocks bigger than 10 kb pinpointing a triplication feature that can be observed in several chromosome segments (e.g. Chr07, which has two homolog regions with Chr12 and one with Chr13). These blocks have an average size of 65 kb and the largest duplication consists of a 4.1-Mb block shared between Chr18 and Chr20 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig4">4a</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">5</a>). Reciprocal pairwise comparisons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Wang, Y., Coleman-Derr, D., Chen, G. &amp; Gu, Y. Q. OrthoVenn: a web server for genome wide comparison and annotation of orthologous clusters across multiple species. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, W78–W84 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1413">33</a></sup> of the 38,258 WL genes with 104,607 genes from its closest relative NLL, the model legume <i>Medicago truncatula</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Tang, H. et al. An improved genome release (version Mt4.0) for the model legume Medicago truncatula. BMC Genomics 15, 312 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1420">34</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> identified 25,615 orthologs clusters (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig4">4b</a>). 473 out of these groups contain only WL paralog genes (1242 in total), probably as a result of the predicted genome triplication event (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">6</a>). Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Finn, R. D. et al. InterPro in 2017-beyond protein family and domain annotations. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, D190–D199 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1433">35</a></sup> terms representation revealed an enriched annotation of serine-type carboxypeptidase activity proteins (GO:0004185), however most of the clusters have no GO term associated (58%, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">6</a>). The WL genome shared highly conserved syntenic blocks with the genome of NLL and <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, the reference genome within this family (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Lupin genome intragenomic duplications and genomic synteny."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Lupin genome intragenomic duplications and genomic synteny.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1049" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Syntenic regions inside white lupin genome. The colored lines link colinearity blocks that represent syntenic regions that are bigger than 50 kb. <b>b</b> OrthoMCL clustering of white lupin genes with those of <i>L. angustifolius</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i>.﻿ Numbers in the sections of the diagram indicate the number of clusters (gene groups). ﻿The first number below each species name is the total number of genes of the species, the second number is the number of genes in clusters and the third number is the number of genes that did not cluster.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Soil exploration</h3><p>Most terrestrial plants can form mycorrhizal symbioses that greatly improve mineral nutrition. Lupins however, lost the ability to form such associations (est. 12–14 My) and the ability to form cluster roots appeared ca. 2.5 My ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Skene, K. R. Pattern formation in cluster roots: some developmental and evolutionary considerations. Ann. Bot. 85, 901–908 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1479">36</a></sup>. The former was accompanied by the loss of all mycorrhizal specific genes in the WL genome whereas common symbiotic genes remained functional (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">10</a>, Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">6</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">8</a>). This suggests that WL favored a new type of root adaptive mechanism towards nutrient acquisition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Lambers, H., Clements, J. C. &amp; Nelson, M. N. How a phosphorus-acquisition strategy based on carboxylate exudation powers the success and agronomic potential of lupines (Lupinus, Fabaceae). Am. J. Bot. 100, 263–288 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1496">11</a></sup>. Despite the importance of cluster roots, no gene controlling their development has been described to date. We therefore generated a detailed transcriptomic dataset of WL cluster root developmental zones. Our RNA-seq survey (mRNA and miRNA) covered 8 sections of mature clusters that mimic the temporal stages of their development (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5a</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">7</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Molecular events of root system establishment in white lupin."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Molecular events of root system establishment in white lupin.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="578" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> 8 developmental stages of cluster root development used for transcriptomic studies, showing the formation of numerous rootlets. <b>b</b> Comparative matrix layout of up-regulated genes in the 8 CR segments. Black dots indicate which sample parts (S0 to S7) are grouped and the number of up-regulated genes in the group is indicated on top of each bar. Set size indicates the total number of differentially expressed genes for each sampled fragment. Inserts: heatmap of the 42 genes over expressed in all CR regions (left) and miRNA expressed in the entire CR. <b>c</b> Partial phylogenetic tree of AP2/EREBP subfamily B-1 of Arabidopsis and white lupin orthologs, highlighting the 4 PUCHI orthologs in white lupin. <b>d</b> Left. Visualization of white lupin root system in 2D. Right. Number of lateral roots and cluster roots in GRAECUS (wild) vs. AMIGA (modern) accessions in the first 10 cm of the root system (<i>n</i> = 10). Bar is 5 cm. <b>e</b> Left. Expression pattern of 63 genes that are overexpressed in S1 region, which include <i>LaPUCHI-1</i>. Right. Relative expression of <i>LaPUCHI-1</i> in AMIGA and GRAECUS in top lateral roots of 11-day-old plants. Box edges represent the 0.25 quantile and 0.75 quantile with the median values shown by bold lines. Whiskers extend to data no more than 1.5 times the interquartile range, and remaining data are indicated by dots. Source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5</a>d, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">e</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We produced a matrix representing all intersections of up-regulated (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5b</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">9</a>) and down-regulated (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">12</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">10</a>) genes in the CR parts. Mature rootlets (S6 and S7) showed the highest number of up-regulated genes, compared to an ordinary lateral root, i.e. devoid of cluster roots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5b</a>). This set of genes have a strong enrichment in GO terms associated with membrane components linked with their highly active physiology required to remobilize and acquire phosphate efficiently (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">13</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">15</a>). Interestingly, a list of 42 genes overexpressed in all cluster roots parts (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">11</a>, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5b</a> detail and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">16</a>) showed a strong enrichment in transcription factors (43%) and 9 of them belong to the AP2/EREBP family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Riechmann, J. L. &amp; Meyerowitz, E. M. The AP2/EREBP family of plant transcription factors. Biol. Chem. 379, 633–646 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1582">37</a></sup>. This is a large multigene family, and they are key regulators of several developmental processes, like floral organ identity determination, control of leaf epidermal cell identity and control of lateral root development. In WL, we identified 217 genes in this family.</p><p>Similarly, a list containing only the genes overexpressed in the S1 region, where CR initiation occurs, is also enriched with transcription factors (6 out of 16). There is an overexpression of 3 genes of AP2/EREBP family that are homologs of <i>AtPUCHI</i>, a gene that is required for morphogenesis in the early lateral root primordium of Arabidopsis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Hirota, A., Kato, T., Fukaki, H., Aida, M. &amp; Tasaka, M. The auxin-regulated AP2/EREBP gene PUCHI is required for morphogenesis in the early lateral root primordium of Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 19, 2156–2168 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1592">38</a></sup>. We performed an identification of all the homologs genes of the AP2/EREBP subfamily B-1, to which the gene <i>AtPUCHI</i> belongs. We identified 20 homologs in the white lupin genome and 4 homologs of the gene <i>PUCHI</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5c</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">17</a>).</p><p>In parallel, we identified all mature microRNAs that are expressed in cluster root sections. We identified 103 miRNA cluster families, among which 29 are predictions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5b</a> detail, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">18</a>). Some of the known miRNA families that we identified were already described as related with Pi-deficiency response, such as miRNA156, miRNA166, and miRNA2111<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Hsieh, L.-C. et al. Uncovering small RNA-mediated responses to phosphate deficiency in Arabidopsis by deep sequencing. Plant Physiol. 151, 2120–2132 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1621">39</a></sup>. We also detected members of miRNA399 family, a key regulator for the phosphate starvation response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bari, R., Datt Pant, B., Stitt, M. &amp; Scheible, W.-R. PHO2, MicroRNA399, and PHR1 define a phosphate-signaling pathway in plants. Plant Physiol. 141, 988–999 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1625">40</a></sup>, that were not detected previously in CR of white lupin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Secco, D. et al. Spatio-temporal transcript profiling of rice roots and shoots in response to phosphate starvation and recovery. Plant Cell 25, 4285–4304 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1630">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Zhu, Y. Y. et al. microRNA expression profiles associated with phosphorus deficiency in white lupin (Lupinus albus L.). Plant Sci. 178, 23–29 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1633">42</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">18a</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">12</a>). We identified that 14 genes out of the 42 overexpressed in all cluster root zones are possible miRNA targets, including 5 transcription factors (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">11</a>). Likewise, in the group of 16 genes that are only overexpressed in the region S1, we identified 5 genes that are targets of the detected miRNAs, comprising transcription factors <i>LaWRKY</i> (Lalb_Chr07g0182001) and <i>LaPUCHI-3</i> (Lalb_Chr18g0055601).</p><p>A possible impact of domestication on WL soil exploration capacity was investigated using a 2D-phenotyping platform. We identified that the root system architecture of AMIGA develops earlier than the wild-relative GRAECUS as a result of a strong increase in lateral and cluster root number in the upper part of the root system (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5d</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">19</a>). This difference was correlated with an increased level of expression of the regulatory gene <i>LaPUCHI-1</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig5">5c, e</a>), whose genetic sequence is identical in AMIGA and GRAECUS. A list of candidate genes selected on their high induction level at the S1 stage (<i>LaCLE1</i>, <i>LaMYB1</i>, <i>LaPEP1</i>, <i>LaPME41</i>, and <i>LaSTART</i>), also showed a higher expression level in AMIGA compared to its wild relative GRAECUS (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">20</a>). This suggests that activation of key regulatory genes may trigger the early establishment of the root system, a trait that has been characterized in other crops to be key for more efficient phosphate acquisition (<i>e.g</i>. the <i>pup1</i> QTL in rice where the <i>PSTOL1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gamuyao, R. et al. The protein kinase Pstol1 from traditional rice confers tolerance of phosphorus deficiency. Nature 488, 535–539 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1696">43</a></sup> gene controls early root system establishment).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Seed quality</h3><p>We compared seed protein composition between the AMIGA reference accession, the Ethiopian landrace (P27174) and the wild GRAECUS relative by quantitative 1D gel analysis followed by mass spectrometry identification of specific protein bands (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig6">6b</a>, Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">8</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">21</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">13</a>). AMIGA seeds displayed a disappearance of high molecular weight ß-conglutins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig6">6c</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">14</a>), which are precursor forms normally synthesized in developing cotyledons to give rise to mature polypeptides of lower molecular weight. Their degradation starts as part of the germination process and the appearance of simpler forms in the domesticated variety AMIGA could be linked with its increased seed vigor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Dobiesz, M. &amp; Piotrowicz-Cieślak, A. I. Proteins in relation to vigor and viability of white lupin (Lupinus albus L.) seed stored for 26 years. Front. Plant Sci. 8, 1–11 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1727">44</a></sup>. The long chain ß-conglutins present in the wild accession is also associated with a high allergenicity of lupin seeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jimenez-Lopez, J. C. et al. Characterization of narrow-leaf lupin (Lupinus angustifolius L.) recombinant major allergen IgE-binding proteins and the natural β-conglutin counterparts in sweet lupin seed species. Food Chem. 244, 60–70 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1731">45</a></sup>, a trait that might have been counter-selected during domestication.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="White lupin seed protein and leaf alkaloid content."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: White lupin seed protein and leaf alkaloid content.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-14197-9/MediaObjects/41467_2019_14197_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="391" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Grain appearance in GRAECUS (wild-type), Ethiopian landrace P27174 and <i>cv</i>. AMIGA. Bar is 0.5 cm. <b>b</b> Seed protein composition of AMIGA (DB, dark brown seeds; LB, light brown seeds), P27174 and GRAECUS (A and B: two independent extractions of proteins using Tris-SDS, separation in 12% SDS-PAGE). The bands extracted for MS/MS analysis are highlighted with red arrows. <b>c</b> Protein content quantification of each band extracted for each accession expressed in normalized gel volume. Data are presented as means ± SD, <i>n</i> = 2. <b>d</b> Abundance of the three major alkaloids in young leaf (3- and 5-leaf growth stage), stem and root tissues of the three accessions of <i>L. albus</i> as measured by LC-MS, and expression of the known alkaloid biosynthesis genes in young leaves as measured by qRT-PCR. <i>n</i> = 5 for AMIGA and <i>n</i> = 4 for P27174 and GRAECUS. <b>e</b> Putative biosynthetic pathway of tetracyclic quinolizidine alkaloids in lupins. Characterized steps and respective enzyme names are marked in red. More information about these analyses is described in Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">9</a>. LDC: lysine decarboxylase; CAO: copper amine oxidase; HLT: 13-hydroxylupanine O-tigloyltransferase. Data are presented as means ± SD, <i>n</i> = 4. Source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig6">6</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM6">d</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Measurement of the abundance of key alkaloids in various organs revealed that the modern accession AMIGA has very low levels of lupanine, 13-hydroxylupanine and 13-tigloyloxylupanine (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig6">6d</a>) but we were not able to match this lack of alkaloids with reduced expression of candidate genes involved in the pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#Fig6">6d, e</a>, Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">15</a>). Instead, we identified a list of candidate genes on Chr18 as associated to the <i>pauper</i> QTL that is responsible for the sweet trait of modern WL accessions, including AMIGA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Książkiewicz, M. et al. A high-density consensus linkage map of white lupin highlights synteny with narrow-leafed lupin and provides markers tagging key agronomic traits. Sci. Rep. 7, 15335 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1802">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Lin, R. et al. Development of a sequence-specific PCR marker linked to the gene ‘pauper’ conferring low-alkaloids in white lupin (Lupinus albus L.) for marker assisted selection. Mol. Breed. 23, 153–161 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d15561e1805">46</a></sup> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-14197-9#MOESM1">9</a>).
